  85         </fractions>
  86 <!--
  87 Note: When reading these dates, you should read them as inclusive date ranges.
  88 The date ranges are like date ranges that you see on tomb stones,
  89 but some dates are approximate. Some date ranges overlap due to
  90 revolts, invasions, new constitutions, and slow planned adoption of new currency.
  91 The length of time for these change overs vary widely.
  92 Sometimes, hyperinflation will cause more than one currency to be in effect
  93 in one year. Dates without a "to" attribute mean that the currency is still
  94 in use.
  95 
  96 Dates try to use ISO-8601 style dates, and be accurate as possible.
  97 They are usually in one of the following formats.
  98 yyyy-MM-dd
  99 yyyy-MM
 100 yyyy
 101 
 102 It is also worth noting this as a part of the history of ISO-4217. This
 103 should be kept in mind in case anyone is claiming that an ISO code existed
 104 before 1978 (source: www.unece.org):
 105 ... the International Organization for Standardization (ISO) decided in 1973 to
 106 develop a code for the representation of currencies and funds for use in any
 107 application of trade, commerce or banking. ... At its seventeenth session
 108 (February 1978) the Group of Experts agreed that the three-letter alphabetic
 109 codes for International Standard ISO 4217, "Codes for the representation of
 110 currencies and funds", would be suitable for use in international trade.
 111 
 112 Most of this list was derived from the following sources:
 113 http://www.iso.org/iso/en/prods-services/popstds/currencycodeslist.html
 114 http://www.globalfindata.com/gh/index.html
 115 http://europa.eu.int/comm/translation/currencies/entable1.htm
 116 http://europa.eu.int/comm/budget/inforeuro/index.cfm?fuseaction=countries&Language=en
 117 http://www.unece.org/cefact/recommendations/rec09/rec09_ecetrd203.pdf
 118 The printed version of ISO-4217:2001
 119 
 120 -->
